     <p>
      <b>
       Empress Dowager Cixi
      </b>
      <sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Cixi#Names_of_Empress_Dowager_Cixi" title="Empress Dowager Cixi">
        1
       </a>
      </sup>
      (
      <b>
       Empress Dowager Tzu-hsi
      </b>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
       pinyin
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
        Cx Tihu
       </span>
      </i>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="WadeGiles">
       WadeGiles
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile" xml:lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">
        Tz'u
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        -hsi
        <sup>
         3
        </sup>
        T'ai
        <sup>
         4
        </sup>
        -hou
        <sup>
         4
        </sup>
       </span>
      </i>
      ;
      <small>
       Mandarin pronunciation:
      </small>
      <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
       <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Mandarin" title="Help:IPA for Mandarin">
        [ts t x]
       </a>
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Manchu_language" title="Manchu language">
       Manchu
      </a>
      : Tsysi taiheo; 29 November 1835  15 November 1908), of the
      <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">
       Manchu
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nara_(clan)" title="Nara (clan)">
       Yehenara
      </a>
      clan, was the
      <a href="/wiki/Empress_dowager" title="Empress dowager">
       empress dowager
      </a>
      of China who effectively controlled the Chinese government for 47 years, from 1861 until her death in 1908.
     </p>
     <p>
      Selected as an imperial
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concubine" title="Concubine">
       concubine
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Xianfeng_Emperor" title="Xianfeng Emperor">
       Xianfeng Emperor
      </a>
      in her adolescence, she gave birth to a son in 1856. With Xianfeng's death in 1861, the young boy became the
      <a href="/wiki/Tongzhi_Emperor" title="Tongzhi Emperor">
       Tongzhi Emperor
      </a>
      , and she became
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager" title="Empress Dowager">
       Empress Dowager
      </a>
      . Cixi ousted a group of regents appointed by the late emperor and assumed regency, which she shared with the
      <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Ci%27an" title="Empress Dowager Ci'an">
       Empress Dowager Ci'an
      </a>
      . Cixi then consolidated control over the dynasty when she installed her nephew as the
      <a href="/wiki/Guangxu_Emperor" title="Guangxu Emperor">
       Guangxu Emperor
      </a>
      at the death of the Tongzhi Emperor in 1875, contrary to the traditional
      <a href="/wiki/Order_of_succession" title="Order of succession">
       rules of succession
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">
       Qing dynasty
      </a>
      that had ruled China since 1644. Although she refused to adopt Western models of government, she supported technological and
      <a href="/wiki/New_Army" title="New Army">
       military reforms
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Self-Strengthening_Movement" title="Self-Strengthening Movement">
       Self-Strengthening Movement
      </a>
      . Cixi rejected the
      <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days%27_Reform" title="Hundred Days' Reform">
       Hundred Days' Reforms
      </a>
      of 1898 as impractical and detrimental to dynastic power and placed the Guangxu Emperor under house arrest for supporting reformers. After the
      <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Rebellion" title="Boxer Rebellion">
       Boxer Rebellion
      </a>
      and the invasion of
      <a href="/wiki/Eight-Nation_Alliance" title="Eight-Nation Alliance">
       Allied armies
      </a>
      , external and internal pressures led Cixi to effect institutional changes of just the sort she had resisted and to appoint reform-minded officials. They inaugurated a
      <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">
       Republic
      </a>
      on 1 January 1912 after the dynasty collapsed in the
      <a href="/wiki/Xinhai_Revolution" title="Xinhai Revolution">
       Xinhai Revolution
      </a>
      of 1911, three years after her death.
     </p>
     <p>
      Many historians both in China and abroad have portrayed her as a despot responsible for the fall of the Qing dynasty. Others have suggested that her opponents among the reformers succeeded in making her a
      <a href="/wiki/Scapegoat" title="Scapegoat">
       scapegoat
      </a>
      for problems beyond her control. Furthermore, they claim that she intervened decisively to prevent political disorder, was no more ruthless than other rulers of her time, and that she was an effective reformer in the last years of her life, even if she was reluctant to take on this role.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">

       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E3%80%8A%E5%96%9C%E6%BA%A2%E7%A7%8B%E5%BA%AD%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B%E9%9D%99%E8%B4%B5%E5%A6%83%E9%83%A8%E5%88%86.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        An early portrait of the old
        <a href="/wiki/Empress_Xiaojingcheng" title="Empress Xiaojingcheng">
         Dowager Consort Kangci
        </a>
        , foster mother of the
        <a href="/wiki/Xianfeng_Emperor" title="Xianfeng Emperor">
         Xianfeng Emperor
        </a>
        . She hosted the selection of Xianfeng's consorts in 1851, in which Cixi participated as a potential candidate.

     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cixi%27s_Palace.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="325" data-file-width="410" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4d/Cixi%27s_Palace.jpg/220px-Cixi%27s_Palace.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4d/Cixi%27s_Palace.jpg/330px-Cixi%27s_Palace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/4d/Cixi%27s_Palace.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cixi%27s_Palace.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The Pavilion of Beautiful Scenery, inside which Cixi gave birth to the future
        <a href="/wiki/Tongzhi_Emperor" title="Tongzhi Emperor">
         Tongzhi Emperor
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Empress Cixi was born in the winter of 1835. According to the information listed on a red sheet (File No. 1247) in the "Miscellaneous Pieces of the Palace" (a Qing dynasty documentation package retrieved from the First Historical Archives of China), Cixi was the daughter of Huizheng, an ordinary official from the
      <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">
       Manchu
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nara_(clan)" title="Nara (clan)">
       Yehenara
      </a>
      clan. Palace archives also show that Huizheng was a member of Bordered Blue Banner of the
      <a href="/wiki/Eight_Banners" title="Eight Banners">
       Eight Banners
      </a>
      and was working in Beijing during the year of Cixi's birth, an indication that she was born in Beijing. The file records the location of Cixi's childhood home: Firewood Alley of West Sipailou, Beijing (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      : ).
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1851, Cixi participated in the selection for consorts to the new
      <a href="/wiki/Xianfeng_Emperor" title="Xianfeng Emperor">
       Xianfeng Emperor
      </a>
      alongside sixty other candidates. Cixi was one of the few candidates chosen to stay. Placed in the 6th rank of consorts, she was styled "Noble Lady Lan" (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      : ). Among the other chosen candidates were Noble Lady Li of the Tatala clan (later
      <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Noble_Consort_Zhuangjing" title="Imperial Noble Consort Zhuangjing">
       Consort Li
      </a>
      ), Concubine Yun of the Wugiya clan, and Concubine Zhen of the Niuhuru clan (later Xianfeng's empress consort).
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1854, Cixi was elevated to the 5th rank of consorts and given the title "Imperial Concubine Yi" (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      : ). In 1855, Cixi became pregnant. On 27 April 1856, she gave birth to
      <a href="/wiki/Tongzhi_Emperor" title="Tongzhi Emperor">
       Zaichun
      </a>
      , the Xianfeng Emperor's only surviving son. Soon afterward, she was elevated to the 4th rank of consorts as"Consort Yi" (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      : ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1857, when her son reached his first birthday, Cixi was elevated to the 3rd rank of consorts as "Noble Consort Yi" (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      : ). This rank placed her second only to the
      <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Ci%27an" title="Empress Dowager Ci'an">
       Empress Ci'an
      </a>
      among the women within Xianfeng's household.
     </p>
     <p>
      Unlike many of the other Manchu women in the imperial household, Cixi was known for her ability to read and write Chinese. This skill granted her numerous opportunities to help the ailing emperor in the governing of the Chinese state on a daily basis. On various occasions, the Xianfeng Emperor had Cixi read palace memorials for him and leave instructions on the memorials according to his will. As a result, Cixi became well-informed about state affairs and the art of governing from the ailing emperor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Death_of_the_Xianfeng_Emperor">
       Death of the Xianfeng Emperor
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Death of the Xianfeng Emperor">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In September 1860, during the closing stages of the
      <a href="/wiki/Second_Opium_War" title="Second Opium War">
       Second Opium War
      </a>
      , the British diplomatic envoy
      <a href="/wiki/Harry_Smith_Parkes" title="Harry Smith Parkes">
       Harry Parkes
      </a>
      was arrested and several western hostages were tortured and executed. In retaliation, British and French troops under the command of
      <a href="/wiki/James_Bruce,_8th_Earl_of_Elgin" title="James Bruce, 8th Earl of Elgin">
       Lord Elgin
      </a>
      attacked Beijing, and by the following month they had burned the Emperor's
      <a href="/wiki/Old_Summer_Palace" title="Old Summer Palace">
       Old Summer Palace
      </a>
      to the ground. The
      <a href="/wiki/Xianfeng_Emperor" title="Xianfeng Emperor">
       Xianfeng Emperor
      </a>
      and his entourage, including Cixi, fled Beijing to Rehe (now
      <a href="/wiki/Chengde" title="Chengde">
       Chengde
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">
       Hebei
      </a>
      ) in Manchuria.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On hearing the news of the destruction of the Old Summer Palace, the Xianfeng Emperor (who was already showing signs of dementia) fell into a depression. He turned heavily to alcohol and drugs and became seriously ill.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He summoned eight of his most prestigious ministers, headed by
      <a href="/wiki/Sushun" title="Sushun">
       Sushun
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Zaiyuan" title="Zaiyuan">
       Zaiyuan
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Duanhua" title="Duanhua">
       Duanhua
      </a>
      , and named them the "Eight Regent Ministers" to direct and support the future emperor. On 22 August 1861, the Xianfeng Emperor died at Rehe Palace in the city of Rehe.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Following_the_Death_of_Xianfeng_Emperor">
       Following the Death of Xianfeng Emperor
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Following the Death of Xianfeng Emperor">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      His heir, the son of Noble Consort Yi (future Empress Dowager Cixi), was only five years old. It is commonly assumed that on his deathbed, the Xianfeng Emperor summoned his Empress and Noble Consort Yi and gave each of them a stamp. He hoped that when his son ascended the throne, the Empress and Noble Consort Yi would cooperate in harmony and help the young emperor to grow and mature together. This may also have been done as a check on the power of the Eight Regents. There is no evidence for this incident, however, and it is unlikely that the emperor ever would have intended the Noble Consort Yi to wield political power. It is possible that the seal, allegedly given as a symbol for the child, was really just a present for Noble Consort Yi (Cixi) herself. Informal seals numbered in the thousands and were not considered political accouterments, rather objects of art commissioned for pleasure by emperors to stamp on items such as paintings, or given as presents to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concubines" title="Concubines">
       concubines
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Upon the death of the Xianfeng Emperor, his
      <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Ci%27an" title="Empress Dowager Ci'an">
       Empress Consort
      </a>
      , aged 25, was elevated to the title
      <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Ci%27an" title="Empress Dowager Ci'an">
       Empress Dowager Ci'an
      </a>
      . She was popularly known as the East Empress Dowager because she lived in the Eastern Zhong-Cui Palace). Noble Consort Yi, aged 27, was elevated to the title Empress Dowager Cixi, popularly known as the West Empress Dowager () because she lived inside the Western Chuxiu Palace.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Xinyou_Coup:_Ousting_Sushun">
       Xinyou Coup: Ousting Sushun
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Xinyou Coup: Ousting Sushun">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E3%80%8A%E7%92%87%E9%97%B1%E6%97%A5%E6%B0%B8%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2465" data-file-width="1300" height="322" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/%E3%80%8A%E7%92%87%E9%97%B1%E6%97%A5%E6%B0%B8%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B.jpg/170px-%E3%80%8A%E7%92%87%E9%97%B1%E6%97%A5%E6%B0%B8%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/%E3%80%8A%E7%92%87%E9%97%B1%E6%97%A5%E6%B0%B8%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B.jpg/255px-%E3%80%8A%E7%92%87%E9%97%B1%E6%97%A5%E6%B0%B8%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/%E3%80%8A%E7%92%87%E9%97%B1%E6%97%A5%E6%B0%B8%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B.jpg/340px-%E3%80%8A%E7%92%87%E9%97%B1%E6%97%A5%E6%B0%B8%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E3%80%8A%E7%92%87%E9%97%B1%E6%97%A5%E6%B0%B8%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Portrait of Empress Dowager Ci'an (co-regent with Cixi), with whom Cixi staged the Xinyou Coup.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      By the time of the death of the Xianfeng Emperor, Empress Dowager Cixi had become a shrewd political strategist. In
      <a href="/wiki/Chengde" title="Chengde">
       Rehe
      </a>
      , while waiting for an astrologically favorable time to transport the coffin back to Beijing, Cixi conspired with powerful court officials and imperial relatives to seize power. Cixi's position as the lower-ranked Empress Dowager had no intrinsic political power attached to it. In addition, her son the young emperor was not a political force himself. As a result, it became necessary for her to ally herself with other powerful figures, including the late emperor's principal wife, the
      <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Ci%27an" title="Empress Dowager Ci'an">
       Empress Dowager Ci'an
      </a>
      . Cixi suggested that they become co-reigning Empress Dowagers, with powers exceeding the Eight Regent Ministers; the two had long been close friends since Cixi first came to the imperial household.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edward_Behr_1987.2C_p._45_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Edward_Behr_1987.2C_p._45-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Tensions grew between the two Empresses Dowagers and the Eight Regent Ministers, headed by
      <a href="/wiki/Sushun" title="Sushun">
       Sushun
      </a>
      . The ministers did not appreciate Cixi's interference in political affairs, and their frequent confrontations with the empresses left the
      <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Ci%27an" title="Empress Dowager Ci'an">
       Empress Dowager Ci'an
      </a>
      frustrated. Ci'an often refused to come to court audiences, leaving Empress Dowager Cixi to deal with the ministers alone. Secretly, Cixi had begun gathering the support of talented ministers, soldiers, and others who were ostracized by the Eight Regent Ministers for personal or political reasons. Among them was
      <a href="/wiki/Prince_Gong_(Qing_dynasty)" title="Prince Gong (Qing dynasty)">
       Prince Gong
      </a>
      , who had been excluded from power, yet harbored great ambitions, and
      <a href="/wiki/Yixuan,_Prince_Chun" title="Yixuan, Prince Chun">
       Prince Chun
      </a>
      , the sixth and seventh sons of the Daoguang Emperor, respectively. While Cixi aligned herself with these Princes, a
      <a href="/wiki/Petition" title="Petition">
       memorial
      </a>
      came from
      <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">
       Shandong
      </a>
      asking for her to "listen to politics behind the curtains," i.e., to assume power as
      <a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">
       de facto
      </a>
      ruler. The same petition also asked Prince Gong to enter the political arena as a principal "aide to the Emperor."
     </p>
     <p>
      When the Xianfeng Emperor's funeral procession left for Beijing, Cixi took advantage of her alliances with Princes Gong and Chun. She and the boy emperor returned to the capital before the rest of the party, along with Zaiyuan and Duanhua, two of the principal regents, while Sushun was left to accompany the deceased emperor's procession. Cixi's early return to Beijing meant that she had more time to plan with Prince Gong and ensure that the power base of the Eight Regent Ministers was divided between Sushun and his allies, Zaiyuan and Duanhua. In order to remove them from power, history was re-written: the Regents were dismissed for having carried out incompetent negotiations with the "barbarians" that had caused the Xianfeng Emperor to flee to Rehe "greatly against his will," among other charges.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edward_Behr_1987.2C_p._45_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Edward_Behr_1987.2C_p._45-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      To display her high moral standards, Cixi executed only three of the eight regent ministers. Prince Gong had suggested that Sushun and others be executed by the most painful method, known as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slow_slicing" title="Slow slicing">
       slow slicing
      </a>
      , but Cixi declined the suggestion and ordered that Sushun be beheaded, while the other two also marked for execution, Zaiyuan and Duanhua, were given white silks to allow them to commit suicide. In addition, Cixi refused outright the idea of executing the family members of the ministers, as would be done in accordance with imperial tradition of an alleged usurper. Ironically, Qing imperial tradition also dictated that women and princes were never to engage in politics. In breaking with tradition, Cixi became the only Qing Dynasty Empress to rule from "behind the curtains" ().
     </p>
     <p>
      This palace coup is known as the "Xinyou Palace Coup" (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ) in China after the name of the year 1861 in the
      <a href="/wiki/Sexagenary_cycle" title="Sexagenary cycle">
       Sexagenary cycle
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Behind_the_curtains">
       Behind the curtains
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Behind the curtains">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="New_era">
       New era
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: New era">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In November 1861, a few days following her
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'tat">
        coup d'tat
       </a>
      </i>
      , Cixi was quick to reward Yixin, the Prince Gong, for his help. He was made head of the General Affairs Office and the Internal Affairs Office, and his daughter was made a
      <i>
       Gurun
      </i>
      Princess, a title usually bestowed only on the Empress's first-born daughter. However, Cixi avoided giving Yixin the absolute political power that princes such as
      <a href="/wiki/Dorgon" title="Dorgon">
       Dorgon
      </a>
      exercised during the
      <a href="/wiki/Shunzhi_Emperor" title="Shunzhi Emperor">
       Shunzhi Emperor
      </a>
      's reign. As one of the first acts from "behind the curtains," Cixi (nominally along with Ci'an) issued two important Imperial Edicts on behalf of the emperor. The first stated that the two Empresses Dowager were to be the sole decision makers "without interference," and the second changed the boy emperor's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Era_name" title="Era name">
       era name
      </a>
      from
      <b>
       Qixiang
      </b>
      (; "Auspicious") to
      <b>
       Tongzhi
      </b>
      (; "collective stable").
     </p>
     <p>
      Despite being the sole decision makers, both Ci'an and Cixi were forced to rely on the
      <a href="/wiki/Grand_Council_(Qing_dynasty)" title="Grand Council (Qing dynasty)">
       Grand Council
      </a>
      and a complex series of procedures in order to deal with affairs of state. When state documents came in, they were to be first forwarded to the empresses, then referred back to the prince adviser and the Grand Council. Having discussed the matters, the prince and his colleagues would seek the instruction of the empresses at audiences and imperial orders would be drawn up accordingly, with drafts having to be approved by the empresses before edicts were issued.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It also seems that the most important role of the empresses during the regency was merely to apply their seals to edicts, a merely mechanical role in a complex bureaucracy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cleaning_up_the_bureaucracy">
       Cleaning up the bureaucracy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cleaning up the bureaucracy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Cixi's ascendancy as the absolute power figure in China came at a time of internal chaos and foreign challenges. The effects of the
      <a href="/wiki/Second_Opium_War" title="Second Opium War">
       Second Opium War
      </a>
      were still hovering over the country, and the
      <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">
       Taiping Rebellion
      </a>
      continued its seemingly unstoppable advance through China's south, eating up the Qing Empire bit by bit. Internally, both the national bureaucracy and regional authorities were infested with corruption. 1861 happened to be the year of official examinations, whereby officials of all levels presented their political reports from the previous three years. Cixi decided that the time was ripe for a bureaucratic overhaul, and she personally sought audience with all officials above the level of provincial governor, who had to report to her personally. Cixi thus took on part of the role usually given to the
      <i>
       Bureaucratic Affairs Department
      </i>
      (). Cixi had two prominent officials executed to serve as examples for others: Qingying, a military
      <i>
       shilang
      </i>
      who had tried to bribe his way out of demotion, and He Guiqing, then
      <a href="/wiki/Viceroy_of_Liangjiang" title="Viceroy of Liangjiang">
       Viceroy of Liangjiang
      </a>
      , who fled
      <a href="/wiki/Changzhou" title="Changzhou">
       Changzhou
      </a>
      in the wake of an incoming Taiping army instead of trying to defend the city.
     </p>
     <p>
      Another significant challenge Cixi faced was the increasingly decrepit state of the country's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manchu" title="Manchu">
       Manchu
      </a>
      elite. Since the beginning of the dynasty in 1644, most major positions at court had been held by Manchus, and the emperors had generally shown contempt for the majority
      <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">
       Han Chinese
      </a>
      . Cixi, again in a reversal of imperial tradition, entrusted the country's most powerful military unit against the Taiping army into the hands of a Han Chinese,
      <a href="/wiki/Zeng_Guofan" title="Zeng Guofan">
       Zeng Guofan
      </a>
      . Additionally, in the next three years, Cixi appointed Han Chinese officials to become governors of all southern Chinese provinces, raising alarm bells in an administration traditionally protective of Manchu dominance.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Taiping_victory_and_Prince_Gong">
       Taiping victory and Prince Gong
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Taiping victory and Prince Gong">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prince_Gong_2.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1229" data-file-width="856" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Prince_Gong_2.JPG/170px-Prince_Gong_2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Prince_Gong_2.JPG/255px-Prince_Gong_2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Prince_Gong_2.JPG/340px-Prince_Gong_2.JPG 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prince_Gong_2.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Photograph of Prince Gong, Cixi's crucial ally during the Xinyou Coup. He was rewarded by Cixi for his help during her most difficult times, but was eventually eliminated from office by Cixi for his ambition.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E8%8D%A3%E5%AF%BF%E5%9B%BA%E4%BC%A6%E5%85%AC%E4%B8%BB.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="622" data-file-width="832" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/%E8%8D%A3%E5%AF%BF%E5%9B%BA%E4%BC%A6%E5%85%AC%E4%B8%BB.jpg/220px-%E8%8D%A3%E5%AF%BF%E5%9B%BA%E4%BC%A6%E5%85%AC%E4%B8%BB.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/%E8%8D%A3%E5%AF%BF%E5%9B%BA%E4%BC%A6%E5%85%AC%E4%B8%BB.jpg/330px-%E8%8D%A3%E5%AF%BF%E5%9B%BA%E4%BC%A6%E5%85%AC%E4%B8%BB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/%E8%8D%A3%E5%AF%BF%E5%9B%BA%E4%BC%A6%E5%85%AC%E4%B8%BB.jpg/440px-%E8%8D%A3%E5%AF%BF%E5%9B%BA%E4%BC%A6%E5%85%AC%E4%B8%BB.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E8%8D%A3%E5%AF%BF%E5%9B%BA%E4%BC%A6%E5%85%AC%E4%B8%BB.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Photograph of Princess Rongshou (center seated), daughter of Prince Gong. As a way to show gratitude to the Prince, Cixi adopted his daughter, and elevated her to the rank of Kurun Princess (the highest rank for imperial princesses).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Under the command of General Zeng Guofan, the victorious
      <a href="/wiki/Xiang_Army" title="Xiang Army">
       Xiang Army
      </a>
      defeated the
      <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Heavenly_Kingdom" title="Taiping Heavenly Kingdom">
       Taiping
      </a>
      army in a hard-fought battle at Tianjing (present-day
      <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">
       Nanjing
      </a>
      ) in July 1864. Zeng Guofan was rewarded with the title of "Marquess Yiyong, First Class," and his brother Zeng Guoquan, along with
      <a href="/wiki/Li_Hongzhang" title="Li Hongzhang">
       Li Hongzhang
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Zuo_Zongtang" title="Zuo Zongtang">
       Zuo Zongtang
      </a>
      , all Han Chinese generals from the war, were rewarded respectively with auspicious decorations and titles. With the Taiping threat receding, Cixi focused her attention on new internal threats to her power. Of special concern was the position of Yixin, the
      <a href="/wiki/Prince_Gong_(Qing_dynasty)" title="Prince Gong (Qing dynasty)">
       Prince Gong
      </a>
      , and the Chief Policy Advisor () at Court. Yixin, whose loyalties stretched at least half of the country, also had effectively gathered under his command the support of all outstanding Han Chinese armies. In addition, Yixin controlled daily court affairs as the first-in-charge at the
      <a href="/wiki/Grand_Council_(Qing_dynasty)" title="Grand Council (Qing dynasty)">
       Grand Council
      </a>
      as well as the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Zongli_Yamen" title="Zongli Yamen">
        Zongli Yamen
       </a>
      </i>
      , the
      <i>
       de facto
      </i>
      ministry of foreign affairs. With his increasing stature, Yixin was considered a serious threat to Cixi and her power.
     </p>
     <p>
      Although the prince was rewarded for his conduct and recommendation of Zeng Guofan before the Taiping defeat, Cixi was quick to move after
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cai_Shaoqi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cai Shaoqi (page does not exist)">
       Cai Shaoqi
      </a>
      , a little-known official who was the recorder at court, filed a
      <a href="/wiki/Petition" title="Petition">
       memorial
      </a>
      asking for Yixin's resignation. Having built up a powerful base and a network of allies at court, Yixin considered the memorial insignificant. Cixi, however, took the memorial as a stepping stone to Yixin's removal. In April 1865, under the pretext that Yixin had "improper court conduct before the two empresses," among a series of other charges, Yixin was dismissed from all his positions, but was allowed to keep his title.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The dismissal surprised the nobility and court officials and brought about numerous petitions for his return.
      <a href="/wiki/Yicong" title="Yicong">
       Yicong
      </a>
      , Prince Tun, as well as Yixuan, the
      <a href="/wiki/Yixuan,_Prince_Chun" title="Yixuan, Prince Chun">
       Prince Chun
      </a>
      , both sought their brother's reinstatement. Yixin himself, in an audience with the two empresses, burst into tears.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Bowing to popular pressure, Cixi allowed Yixin to return to his position as the head of the foreign ministry, but rid him of his title of Chief Policy Advisor. Yixin would never return to political prominence again, and neither would the liberal and pro-reform policies of his time. Yixin's demotion revealed Cixi's iron grip on Qing politics, and her lack of willingness to give up absolute power to anyone, not even her most important ally in the Xinyou coup, Prince Gong.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_influence">
       Foreign influence
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Foreign influence">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      China's loss in the
      <a href="/wiki/Second_Opium_War" title="Second Opium War">
       Second Opium War
      </a>
      of 1856 to 1860 was undoubtedly a wake-up call for its imperial rulers. Cixi presided over a country whose military strategies were vastly outdated, both on land and sea and in terms of weaponry. Sensing an immediate threat from foreigners and realizing that China's agricultural-based economy could not hope to compete with the industrial prowess of the West, Cixi made the decision that for the first time in Imperial Chinese history, China would learn from Western powers and import their knowledge and technology. At the time, three prominent Han Chinese officials,
      <a href="/wiki/Zeng_Guofan" title="Zeng Guofan">
       Zeng Guofan
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Li_Hongzhang" title="Li Hongzhang">
       Li Hongzhang
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Zuo_Zongtang" title="Zuo Zongtang">
       Zuo Zongtang
      </a>
      , had all begun industrial programs in the country's southern regions. In supporting these programs, Cixi also decreed the opening of
      <a href="/wiki/Tongwen_Guan" title="Tongwen Guan">
       Tongwen Guan
      </a>
      in 1862, a university-like institution in Beijing that hired foreigners as teachers. Tongwen Guan specialized in new-age topics such as astronomy and mathematics, as well as the English, French, and Russian languages. Groups of young boys were also sent abroad to the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
       United States
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      China's "learn from foreigners" program quickly met with impediments. China's military institutions were in desperate need of reform. Cixi's solution, under the advice of officials at court, was to purchase seven British warships. When the warships arrived in China, however, they were staffed with British sailors, all under British command. The Chinese were enraged at this "international joke," negotiations broke down between the two parties, and China returned the warships to Britain, where they were to be auctioned off. Scholars sometimes attribute the failure of China's foreign programs to Cixi's conservative attitude and old methods of thinking, and contend that Cixi would learn only so much from the foreigners, provided it did not infringe upon her own power. Under the pretext that a railway was too loud and would "disturb the Emperor's tombs," Cixi forbade its construction. When construction went ahead anyway in 1877 on Li Hongzhang's recommendation, Cixi asked that they be pulled by horse-drawn carts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Cixi was especially alarmed at the liberal thinking of people who had studied abroad, and saw that it posed a new threat to her power. In 1881, Cixi put a halt to the policy of sending children abroad to study and withdrew her formerly open attitude towards foreigners.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Tongzhi.27s_marriage">
       Tongzhi's marriage
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Tongzhi's marriage">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E3%80%8A%E5%AD%9D%E5%93%B2%E6%AF%85%E7%9A%87%E5%90%8E%E6%9C%9D%E6%9C%8D%E5%83%8F%E3%80%8B%E5%B1%80%E9%83%A8.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="670" data-file-width="670" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/%E3%80%8A%E5%AD%9D%E5%93%B2%E6%AF%85%E7%9A%87%E5%90%8E%E6%9C%9D%E6%9C%8D%E5%83%8F%E3%80%8B%E5%B1%80%E9%83%A8.jpg/220px-%E3%80%8A%E5%AD%9D%E5%93%B2%E6%AF%85%E7%9A%87%E5%90%8E%E6%9C%9D%E6%9C%8D%E5%83%8F%E3%80%8B%E5%B1%80%E9%83%A8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/%E3%80%8A%E5%AD%9D%E5%93%B2%E6%AF%85%E7%9A%87%E5%90%8E%E6%9C%9D%E6%9C%8D%E5%83%8F%E3%80%8B%E5%B1%80%E9%83%A8.jpg/330px-%E3%80%8A%E5%AD%9D%E5%93%B2%E6%AF%85%E7%9A%87%E5%90%8E%E6%9C%9D%E6%9C%8D%E5%83%8F%E3%80%8B%E5%B1%80%E9%83%A8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/%E3%80%8A%E5%AD%9D%E5%93%B2%E6%AF%85%E7%9A%87%E5%90%8E%E6%9C%9D%E6%9C%8D%E5%83%8F%E3%80%8B%E5%B1%80%E9%83%A8.jpg/440px-%E3%80%8A%E5%AD%9D%E5%93%B2%E6%AF%85%E7%9A%87%E5%90%8E%E6%9C%9D%E6%9C%8D%E5%83%8F%E3%80%8B%E5%B1%80%E9%83%A8.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E3%80%8A%E5%AD%9D%E5%93%B2%E6%AF%85%E7%9A%87%E5%90%8E%E6%9C%9D%E6%9C%8D%E5%83%8F%E3%80%8B%E5%B1%80%E9%83%A8.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Portrait of Empress Xiaozheyi, also known as Empress Jiashun and "Lady Alute", who had the approval of Empress Dowager Ci'an but never Cixi's. It is widely speculated that the Empress was pregnant with Tongzhi's child and that Cixi orchestrated the Lady's demise.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_-_Ceremonial_Headdress_-_Walters_863_-_Back.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1799" data-file-width="1714" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Chinese_-_Ceremonial_Headdress_-_Walters_863_-_Back.jpg/220px-Chinese_-_Ceremonial_Headdress_-_Walters_863_-_Back.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Chinese_-_Ceremonial_Headdress_-_Walters_863_-_Back.jpg/330px-Chinese_-_Ceremonial_Headdress_-_Walters_863_-_Back.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Chinese_-_Ceremonial_Headdress_-_Walters_863_-_Back.jpg/440px-Chinese_-_Ceremonial_Headdress_-_Walters_863_-_Back.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_-_Ceremonial_Headdress_-_Walters_863_-_Back.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Ceremonial headdress likely worn by Cixi. The small phoenixes emerging from the surface represent the empress.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
         <a href="#cite_note-13">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          13
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        The Walters Art Museum
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1872, the Tongzhi Emperor turned 17. Under the guidance of the
      <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Ci%27an" title="Empress Dowager Ci'an">
       Empress Dowager Ci'an
      </a>
      , he was married to
      <a href="/wiki/Empress_Xiaozheyi" title="Empress Xiaozheyi">
       Empress Jiashun
      </a>
      . Empress Jiashun's grandfather,
      <a href="/wiki/Duanhua" title="Duanhua">
       Prince Zheng
      </a>
      , was one of the eight ministers selected by Xianfeng to guide Tongzhi. He had been Cixi's enemy during the Xinyou Coup and was ordered to commit suicide after Cixi's victory. As a consequence, there were tensions between Cixi and Empress Jiashun, and this was often a source of irritation for Cixi. Moreover, the empress's zodiac symbol of tiger was perceived as life-threatening by the superstitious Cixi, whose own zodiac symbol was a goat. According to Cixi's belief, it was a warning from God that she would eventually fall prey to the empress.
     </p>
     <p>
      As the principal consort of the Emperor, Empress Jiashun was well received by both Tongzhi and the Empress Dowager Ci'an. Her personal consultants once warned her to be more agreeable and docile to Cixi, as Cixi was the figure who truly held the power. She replied: "I am a principal consort, having been carried through the front gate with pomp and circumstance, as mandated by our ancestors. Empress Dowager Cixi was a concubine, and entered our household through a side gate."
     </p>
     <p>
      Since the very beginning of his marriage, the emperor proceeded to spend most of his time with his empress at the expense of his four concubines, including the
      <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Noble_Consort_Shushen" title="Imperial Noble Consort Shushen">
       Imperial Noble Consort Shushen
      </a>
      , who was the empress intended by Cixi. As hostility grew between Cixi and Empress Jiashun, Cixi suggested the couple spend more time on studies and spied on Tongzhi using
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eunuch_(court_official)" title="Eunuch (court official)">
       eunuchs
      </a>
      . After her warning was ignored, Cixi ordered the couple to separate, and Tongzhi purportedly spent several months following Cixi's order in isolation at
      <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Heavenly_Purity" title="Palace of Heavenly Purity">
       Qianqing Palace
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The young emperor, who could no longer cope with his grief and loneliness, grew more and more ill-tempered. He began to treat his servants with cruelty and punished them physically for minor offences. Under the joined influence of court eunuchs and Zaicheng, eldest son of Prince Gong and Tongzhi's contemporary and best friend, Tongzhi managed to escape the palace in search of pleasure in the unrestricted parts of Beijing. For several evenings the emperor disguised himself as a commoner and secretly spent the nights in the brothels of Beijing. The emperor's sexual habits became common talk among court officials and commoners, and there are many records of Tongzhi's escapades.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Tongzhi.27s_deficiencies_in_ruling">
       Tongzhi's deficiencies in ruling
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Tongzhi's deficiencies in ruling">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E3%80%8A%E6%B8%B8%E8%89%BA%E6%80%A1%E6%83%85%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3353" data-file-width="2000" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/%E3%80%8A%E6%B8%B8%E8%89%BA%E6%80%A1%E6%83%85%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B.jpg/170px-%E3%80%8A%E6%B8%B8%E8%89%BA%E6%80%A1%E6%83%85%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/%E3%80%8A%E6%B8%B8%E8%89%BA%E6%80%A1%E6%83%85%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B.jpg/255px-%E3%80%8A%E6%B8%B8%E8%89%BA%E6%80%A1%E6%83%85%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/%E3%80%8A%E6%B8%B8%E8%89%BA%E6%80%A1%E6%83%85%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B.jpg/340px-%E3%80%8A%E6%B8%B8%E8%89%BA%E6%80%A1%E6%83%85%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E3%80%8A%E6%B8%B8%E8%89%BA%E6%80%A1%E6%83%85%E5%9B%BE%E3%80%8B.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Portrait of the Tongzhi Emperor doing his coursework. Cixi's high expectations of him caused his strong distaste for learning.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Tongzhi received a rigorous education from four famous teachers of Cixi's own choosing: Li Hongzao, Qi Junzao, Weng Xincun, and Wo Ren. This group was later joined by Xincun's son
      <a href="/wiki/Weng_Tonghe" title="Weng Tonghe">
       Weng Tonghe
      </a>
      ; the emperor's governor, also selected by Cixi, was Mianyu. The imperial teachers instructed the emperor in the classics and various old texts for which the emperor displayed little or no interest.
     </p>
     <p>
      The pressure and stress put upon the young emperor made him despise learning for the majority of his life. According to Weng Tonghe's diary, the emperor could not read a memorandum in full sentences by age sixteen. Worried about her son's inability to learn, Cixi only pressured Tongzhi more. When he was given personal rule at age 18, in November 1873 (four years behind the usual custom), Tongzhi proved to be an incompetent emperor.
     </p>
     <p>
      Tongzhi made two important policy decisions during his short stint of rule, which lasted from 1873 to 1875. First, he decreed that the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuanmingyuan" title="Yuanmingyuan">
       Imperial Summer Palace
      </a>
      , destroyed by the English and French in the Second Opium War, would be completely rebuilt under the pretext that it was a gift to Cixi and Ci'an. Historians also suggest that it was an attempt to drive Cixi from the
      <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">
       Forbidden City
      </a>
      so that he could rule without interference in policy or his private affairs.
     </p>
     <p>
      The imperial treasury was almost depleted at the time from internal strife and foreign wars, and as a result, Tongzhi asked the Board of Finance to forage for the necessary funds. In addition, he encouraged members of the nobility and high officials to donate funds from their personal resources. Once construction began, Tongzhi checked its progress on a monthly basis, and would often spend days away from court, indulging himself in pleasures outside of the Forbidden City.
     </p>
     <p>
      Uneasy about the emperor's neglect of national affairs, Princes Yixin and Yixuan (
      <a href="/wiki/Yixuan,_Prince_Chun" title="Yixuan, Prince Chun">
       Prince Chun
      </a>
      ), along with the top officials of the imperial court, submitted a joint memorandum asking the Emperor to cease the construction of the Summer Palace, among other recommendations. Tongzhi, unwilling to submit to criticism, issued an Imperial Edict in August 1874 to rid Yixin of his title of prince and be demoted to the status of a commoner. Two days later,
      <a href="/wiki/Yicong" title="Yicong">
       Yicong
      </a>
      , Yixuan, Yihui, Jingshou,
      <a href="/wiki/Yikuang,_Prince_Qing" title="Yikuang, Prince Qing">
       Yikuang
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Wenxiang" title="Wenxiang">
       Wenxiang
      </a>
      , Baoju, and Grand Councillors Shen Guifen and Li Hongzao were all to be stripped of their respective titles and jobs.
     </p>
     <p>
      Seeing the mayhem unfold from behind the scenes, Cixi and Ci'an made an unprecedented appearance at court directly criticizing the emperor for his wrongful actions and asked him to withdraw the Edict; Cixi said that "without Prince Gong, the situation today would not exist for you and me."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Feeling a grand sense of loss at court and unable to assert his authority, the emperor returned to his former habits. It was rumored that he caught
      <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">
       syphilis
      </a>
      and became visibly ill. The doctors spread a rumor that the emperor had caught smallpox, and proceeded to give medical treatment accordingly. Within a few weeks, on 13 January 1875, the emperor died. The Jiashun Empress followed suit in March. Judging from a modern medical perspective, the onset of syphilis comes in stages, thus the emperor's quick death does not seem to reflect its symptoms. Therefore most historians maintain that Tongzhi did, in fact, die from smallpox. Regardless, by 1875, Cixi was back onto the helm of imperial power.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Regency_over_the_Guangxu_Emperor">
       Regency over the Guangxu Emperor
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Regency over the Guangxu Emperor">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="New_challenges_and_illness">
       New challenges and illness
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: New challenges and illness">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_with_Attendant.JPG">

         </a>
        </div>
        The Empress Dowager (front middle) poses with her court attendants and Guangxu's empress (second from left), who was also her niece
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E6%85%88%E7%A6%A7%E4%B8%8E%E5%BA%86%E7%8E%8B%E5%BA%9C%E5%9B%9B%E6%A0%BC%E6%A0%BC.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="646" data-file-width="925" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/%E6%85%88%E7%A6%A7%E4%B8%8E%E5%BA%86%E7%8E%8B%E5%BA%9C%E5%9B%9B%E6%A0%BC%E6%A0%BC.jpg/220px-%E6%85%88%E7%A6%A7%E4%B8%8E%E5%BA%86%E7%8E%8B%E5%BA%9C%E5%9B%9B%E6%A0%BC%E6%A0%BC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/%E6%85%88%E7%A6%A7%E4%B8%8E%E5%BA%86%E7%8E%8B%E5%BA%9C%E5%9B%9B%E6%A0%BC%E6%A0%BC.jpg/330px-%E6%85%88%E7%A6%A7%E4%B8%8E%E5%BA%86%E7%8E%8B%E5%BA%9C%E5%9B%9B%E6%A0%BC%E6%A0%BC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/%E6%85%88%E7%A6%A7%E4%B8%8E%E5%BA%86%E7%8E%8B%E5%BA%9C%E5%9B%9B%E6%A0%BC%E6%A0%BC.jpg/440px-%E6%85%88%E7%A6%A7%E4%B8%8E%E5%BA%86%E7%8E%8B%E5%BA%9C%E5%9B%9B%E6%A0%BC%E6%A0%BC.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E6%85%88%E7%A6%A7%E4%B8%8E%E5%BA%86%E7%8E%8B%E5%BA%9C%E5%9B%9B%E6%A0%BC%E6%A0%BC.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The Empress Dowager holds hands with the fourth daughter of Prince Qing (to her left) and chief palace eunuch Li Lianying (to her right). The lady standing in the background is Consort Jin (later Dowager Consort Duankang).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Tongzhi died without a male heir, a circumstance that created an unprecedented succession crisis in the dynastic line. Members of the generation above were considered unfit, as they could not, by definition, be the successor of their nephew. Therefore, the new Emperor had to be from a generation below or the same generation as Tongzhi. After considerable disagreement between the two Dowagers, Zaitian, the first-born of the
      <a href="/wiki/Yixuan,_Prince_Chun" title="Yixuan, Prince Chun">
       Prince Chun
      </a>
      Yixuan and Cixi's sister, then aged four, was to become the new Emperor. 1875 was declared the era of
      <b>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guangxu" title="Guangxu">
        Guangxu
       </a>
      </b>
      , or the reign of
      <i>
       Glorious Succession
      </i>
      . Young Zaitian was taken from his home and for the remainder of his life would be cut completely off from his family. While addressing Ci'an conventionally as
      <i>
       Huang O'niang
      </i>
      (Empress Mother), Zaitian was forced to address Cixi as
      <i>
       Qin Baba
      </i>
      (; lit. "Biological Dad"), in order to enforce an image that she was the fatherly power figure in the house.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qbb_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-qbb-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Guangxu Emperor began his education when he was aged five, taught by Imperial Tutor
      <a href="/wiki/Weng_Tonghe" title="Weng Tonghe">
       Weng Tonghe
      </a>
      , with whom he would develop a lasting bond.
     </p>
     <p>
      Shortly after the accession of the Emperor Guangxu, Cixi fell severely ill,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      leaving Ci'an to attend to most of the affairs of state.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Cixi was largely inaccessible to her young nephew, as well.
     </p>
     <p>
      The sudden death of Ci'an in April 1881 brought Cixi a new challenge. Ci'an took little interest in running state business, but was the decision maker in most family affairs and as the Emperor Xianfeng's empress, took seniority over Cixi, despite being two years Cixi's junior. Some have argued that there had been a possible conflict between Cixi and Ci'an over the execution of An Dehai or a possible will from the late Xianfeng Emperor issued exclusively to Ci'an and that rumours began circulating at court that Cixi had poisoned Ci'an.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because of a lack of evidence, however, historians are reluctant to believe that Ci'an was poisoned by Cixi, but instead choose to believe that the cause of death was a sudden
      <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">
       stroke
      </a>
      , as validated by
      <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">
       traditional Chinese medicine
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the years between 1881 and 1883, Cixi resorted to communicating with her ministers in writing.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Furthermore, the young Emperor Guangxu reportedly had been forced to conduct some audiences alone, without Cixi.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kwong.2C_pg._54_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kwong.2C_pg._54-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The once fierce and determined Prince Gong, frustrated by Cixi's iron grip on power, did little to question Cixi on state affairs, and supported Manchu involvement in the
      <a href="/wiki/Sino-French_War" title="Sino-French War">
       Sino-French War
      </a>
      . Cixi used China's loss in the war as a pretext for getting rid of Prince Gong and other important decision makers in the Grand Council in 1885. She downgraded him to "advisor," and promoted the more easily influenced
      <a href="/wiki/Yixuan,_Prince_Chun" title="Yixuan, Prince Chun">
       Yixuan, Prince Chun
      </a>
      . After being appointed President of the Navy, Prince Chun, in a sign of unswerving loyalty to Cixi, but in reality a move to protect his son, the new Emperor, moved funds from the military to reconstruct the Imperial Summer Palace outside of Beijing city as a place for Cixi's retirement. Prince Chun did not want Cixi to interfere with his son Guangxu's affairs once he came of age. Cixi showed no opposition to the construction of the palace.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="The_Guangxu_Emperor.27s_accession">
       The Guangxu Emperor's accession
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: The Guangxu Emperor's accession">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Guangxu technically gained the right to rule at the age of 16 in 1887 after Cixi issued an edict for Guangxu to have his accession to rule ceremony. Because of her prestige and power, however, court officials voiced their opposition to Guangxu's personal rule, citing the Emperor's youth as the main reason. Shiduo,
      <a href="/wiki/Yixuan,_Prince_Chun" title="Yixuan, Prince Chun">
       Yixuan
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Weng_Tonghe" title="Weng Tonghe">
       Weng Tonghe
      </a>
      , each with a different motive, asked Guangxu's accession to be postponed until a later date. Cixi, with her reputed reluctance, accepted the "advice" and legitimized her continued rule through a new legal document that allowed her to "aid" the Guangxu Emperor in his rule indefinitely.
     </p>
     <p>
      However, despite her prolonged regency, the Emperor Guangxu began to slowly take on more responsibilities. In 1886, the emperor attended his first field plowing ceremony and also began commenting on imperial state documents and by 1887, he began to rule under Cixi's supervision.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kwong.2C_pg._54_20-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kwong.2C_pg._54-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Emperor Guangxu would eventually marry and take up the reins of power in 1889. By then the Guangxu Emperor was already 18, older than the conventional marital age for Emperors. Prior to the wedding, a large fire engulfed the Gate of Supreme Harmony at the
      <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">
       Forbidden City
      </a>
      , following a trend of natural disasters in recent years, which according to Chinese political theory meant that the current rulers were losing the "
      <a href="/wiki/Mandate_of_Heaven" title="Mandate of Heaven">
       Mandate of Heaven
      </a>
      ".
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E5%85%89%E7%BB%AA%E7%8F%8D%E5%A6%83.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="346" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/%E5%85%89%E7%BB%AA%E7%8F%8D%E5%A6%83.jpg/220px-%E5%85%89%E7%BB%AA%E7%8F%8D%E5%A6%83.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/%E5%85%89%E7%BB%AA%E7%8F%8D%E5%A6%83.jpg/330px-%E5%85%89%E7%BB%AA%E7%8F%8D%E5%A6%83.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/97/%E5%85%89%E7%BB%AA%E7%8F%8D%E5%A6%83.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E5%85%89%E7%BB%AA%E7%8F%8D%E5%A6%83.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Consort Zhen, Guangxu's most beloved consort, was initially liked but eventually hated by Cixi.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      As his empress, Empress Dowager Cixi chose her niece &amp; Guangxu's cousin, Jingfen, who would become the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_Longyu" title="Empress Longyu">
       Empress Longyu
      </a>
      . Cixi also selected two concubines for him who were sisters, Consorts
      <a href="/wiki/Consort_Jin" title="Consort Jin">
       Jin
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Consort_Zhen" title="Consort Zhen">
       Zhen.
      </a>
      Guangxu eventually would prefer to spend more time with Zhen, neglecting his Empress, much to Cixi's dismay. In 1894, Cixi, citing intervention in political affairs as the main reason, degraded Zhen, and according to some reports, had her flogged.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kwong.2C_pg._60_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kwong.2C_pg._60-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Jin had also been implicated in Zhen's reported influence peddling, also apparently suffered a similar punishment
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kwong.2C_pg._60_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kwong.2C_pg._60-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and a cousin of theirs, an official named Zhi Rui, was banished from the capital to a military outpost.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id=".22Retirement.22">
       "Retirement"
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title='Edit section: "Retirement"'>
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      On March 5, 1889, the dowager empress retired her second regency. However, despite no longer being regent, she still was, effectively, head of the imperial family.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kwong.2C_pg._29_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kwong.2C_pg._29-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many officials, furthermore, owing in part to her seniority, along with her personalized approach to court favorites, which had included giving them gifts of her artwork and inviting them to join her at the theater for opera and acrobatics,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      felt and showed more loyalty to the dowager empress than they did to the emperor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Even after Guangxu began formal rule at age 19, Cixi continued to influence his decisions and actions, despite residing for a period of time at the
      <a href="/wiki/Summer_Palace" title="Summer Palace">
       Imperial Summer Palace
      </a>
      which she had ordered Guangxu's father to construct, with the official intention not to intervene in politics. Guangxu paid visits to her, along with the entourage of court officials, every second or third day, where major political decisions would be made. Weng Tonghe observed that while Guangxu dealt with day-to-day administration, in more complex cases, the Grand Councillors gave their advice, and in the most complex cases, they sought the advice of Cixi.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1894, the
      <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">
       First Sino-Japanese War
      </a>
      broke out for control of Korea. During this period, Cixi was continuously called upon to arbitrate in policy decisions, with the emperor even being bypassed in the decision-making process.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Cixi eventually was given copies of the secret palace memorials, as well, and this practice was carried on until 1898 when it was rendered unnecessary.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Cixi, in November 1895, celebrated her sixtieth birthday. Borrowing from the plans for used for celebrations of the seventieth and eightieth birthdays of the
      <a href="/wiki/Empress_Xiaoshengxian" title="Empress Xiaoshengxian">
       mother of the Qianlong Emperor
      </a>
      , plans included a triumphal progress along the decorated road between the Forbidden City and the Summer Palace, decorations of the Beijing city gates and monumental archways, free theatrical performances, remission of punishments and the restoration of degraded officials.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, the war between China and Japan forced the dowager empress to cancel the lavish celebration and a much smaller celebration was held in the Forbidden City.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hundred_Days.27_Reform">
       Hundred Days' Reform
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Hundred Days' Reform">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      After taking power, the
      <a href="/wiki/Guangxu_Emperor" title="Guangxu Emperor">
       Guangxu Emperor
      </a>
      was more reform-minded than the conservative-leaning Empress Dowager Cixi. After a humiliating defeat in the
      <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">
       First Sino-Japanese War
      </a>
      of 1894, during which China's
      <a href="/wiki/Beiyang" title="Beiyang">
       Beiyang
      </a>
      Navy was crushed by the Japanese forces, the Qing government faced numerous unprecedented challenges internally and abroad, with its very existence at stake. Under the influence of reformers
      <a href="/wiki/Kang_Youwei" title="Kang Youwei">
       Kang Youwei
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Liang_Qichao" title="Liang Qichao">
       Liang Qichao
      </a>
      , Guangxu believed that by learning from constitutional monarchies like
      <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">
       Japan
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
       Germany
      </a>
      , China would become more powerful politically and economically. In June 1898, the Guangxu Emperor began the
      <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days%27_Reform" title="Hundred Days' Reform">
       Hundred Days' Reform
      </a>
      (), aimed at a series of sweeping changes politically, legally, and socially. For a brief time, after the supposed retirement of the Empress Dowager Cixi, the Guangxu Emperor issued edicts for a massive number of far-reaching modernizing reforms.
     </p>
     <p>
      The reforms, however, were too sudden for a China still under significant neo-Confucian influence, and displeased Cixi as it served as a serious check on her power. Some government and military officials warned Cixi that the
      <i>
       ming-shih
      </i>
      (reformation bureau) had been geared toward conspiracy. Allegations of treason against the Emperor, as well as suspected Japanese influence within the reform movement, including a suspicious visit from the Japanese Prime Minister, led Empress Dowager Cixi to resume the role of regent and once again take control of the country.
     </p>
     <p>
      In another
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coup_d%27etat" title="Coup d'etat">
        coup d'etat
       </a>
      </i>
      carried out by General
      <a href="/wiki/Ronglu" title="Ronglu">
       Ronglu
      </a>
      's personnel on 21 September 1898, the Guangxu Emperor was taken to Ocean Terrace, a small palace on an island in the middle of
      <a href="/wiki/Zhongnanhai" title="Zhongnanhai">
       Zhongnanhai
      </a>
      linked to the rest of the
      <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">
       Forbidden City
      </a>
      with only a controlled causeway. Empress Dowager Cixi would follow with an edict dictating the Guangxu Emperor's total disgrace and "not being fit to be Emperor". The Guangxu Emperor's reign had effectively come to an end.
     </p>
     <p>
      A crisis followed in the Qing court on the issue of abdication. However, bowing to increasing western pressure and general civil discontent over the issue, Cixi did not forcibly remove Guangxu from the throne, although she attempted crowning Punji, a boy of fourteen who was from a close branch of the Imperial family, as the
      <a href="/wiki/Crown_prince" title="Crown prince">
       crown prince
      </a>
      . The Guangxu era nominally continued until 1908, but the Emperor lost all honours, respect, power, and privileges, including his freedom of movement. Most of his supporters, including his former tutor
      <a href="/wiki/Weng_Tonghe" title="Weng Tonghe">
       Weng Tonghe
      </a>
      , and the man he had recommended,
      <a href="/wiki/Kang_Youwei" title="Kang Youwei">
       Kang Youwei
      </a>
      , were exiled, while six prominent reformers led by
      <a href="/wiki/Tan_Sitong" title="Tan Sitong">
       Tan Sitong
      </a>
      were executed in public by Empress Dowager Cixi. Kang continued to work for a more progressive Qing Empire while in exile, remaining loyal to the Guangxu Emperor and hoping eventually to restore him to power. His efforts would prove to be in vain.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Boxer_Rebellion">
       Boxer Rebellion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Boxer Rebellion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Official_Audience_of_Their_Majesties.jpg">

       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">

        </div>
