        <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Carl" title="Katharine Carl">
         Katharine Carl
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_On_Throne_7.PNG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="752" data-file-width="1011" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/The_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_On_Throne_7.PNG/220px-The_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_On_Throne_7.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/The_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_On_Throne_7.PNG/330px-The_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_On_Throne_7.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/The_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_On_Throne_7.PNG/440px-The_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_On_Throne_7.PNG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_On_Throne_7.PNG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Empress Dowager Cixi and women of the American legation. Holding her hand is Sarah Conger, wife of the American minister to China.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1900, the
      <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Rebellion" title="Boxer Rebellion">
       Boxer Uprising
      </a>
      broke out in northern China. Perhaps fearing further foreign intervention, Cixi threw in her support to these anti-foreign bands, making an official announcement of her support for the movement and a formal declaration of war on the European powers. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manchu" title="Manchu">
       Manchu
      </a>
      General
      <a href="/wiki/Ronglu" title="Ronglu">
       Ronglu
      </a>
      deliberately sabotaged the performance of the Imperial army during the rebellion. Dong Fuxiang's Muslim troops were able and eager to destroy the foreign military forces in the legations, but Ronglu stopped them from doing so.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Manchu prince
      <a href="/wiki/Zaiyi" title="Zaiyi">
       Zaiyi
      </a>
      was xenophobic and was friends with
      <a href="/wiki/Dong_Fuxiang" title="Dong Fuxiang">
       Dong Fuxiang
      </a>
      . Zaiyi wanted artillery for Dong Fuxiang's troops to destroy the legations. Ronglu blocked the transfer of artillery to Zaiyi and Dong, preventing them from destroying the legations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When artillery was finally supplied to the Imperial Army and Boxers, it was only done so in limited amounts; Ronglu deliberately held back the rest of them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Chinese forces defeated the small 2,000 person Western relief force at the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Langfang" title="Battle of Langfang">
       Battle of Langfang
      </a>
      but lost several decisive battles, including the
      <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Beicang" title="Battle of Beicang">
       Battle of Beicang
      </a>
      , and the entire royal court was forced to retreat as the allied forces invaded Beijing. Due to the fact that moderates at the Qing court tried to appease the foreigners by moving the Muslim
      <a href="/wiki/Kansu_Braves" title="Kansu Braves">
       Kansu Braves
      </a>
      out of their way, the Allied army was able to march into Beijing and seize the capital.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      During the war, Cixi displayed concern about China's situation and foreign aggression, saying, "Perhaps their magic is not to be relied upon; but can we not rely on the hearts and minds of the people? Today China is extremely weak. We have only the people's hearts and minds to depend upon. If we cast them aside and lose the people's hearts, what can we use to sustain the country?" The Chinese people were almost unanimous in their support for the Boxers due to the Western Allied invasion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      When Cixi received an ultimatum demanding that China surrender total control over all its military and financial affairs to foreigners,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      she defiantly stated before the entire Grand Council, "Now they [the Powers] have started the aggression, and the extinction of our nation is imminent. If we just fold our arms and yield to them, I would have no face to see our ancestors after death. If we must perish, why not fight to the death?"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It was at this point that Cixi began to blockade the legations with the armies of the
      <a href="/wiki/Peking_Field_Force" title="Peking Field Force">
       Peking Field Force
      </a>
      , which began the siege.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Cixi stated that "I have always been of the opinion, that the allied armies had been permitted to escape too easily in 1860. Only a united effort was then necessary to have given China the victory. Today, at last, the opportunity for revenge has come", and said that millions of Chinese would join the cause of fighting the foreigners since the Manchus had provided "great benefits" on China.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      During the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Peking" title="Battle of Peking">
       Battle of Peking
      </a>
      , the entire Chinese Imperial Court, including the Empress Dowager and Emperor Guangxu, fled Beijing and evacuated to Xi'an in
      <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">
       Shaanxi
      </a>
      province as the allied forced invaded the city. After the fall of Beijing, the allied forces negotiated a treaty with the Qing dynasty, sending messengers to the Dowager Empress in Xi'an. Included in the terms of the agreement was a guarantee that China would not have to give up any further territories to foreign powers. Many of the Dowager Empress's advisers in the Imperial Court insisted that the war against the foreigners be continued. They recommended that
      <a href="/wiki/Dong_Fuxiang" title="Dong Fuxiang">
       Dong Fuxiang
      </a>
      be given responsibility to continue the war effort. The Dowager was practical, however, and decided that the terms were generous enough for her to acquiesce and stop the war, at least after she was assured of her continued reign when the war was concluded.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Western powers needed a government strong enough to suppress further anti-foreign movements, but too weak to act on its own; they supported the continuation of the Qing, rather than allowing it to be overthrown. Cixi turned once more to
      <a href="/wiki/Li_Hongzhang" title="Li Hongzhang">
       Li Hongzhang
      </a>
      to negotiate. Li agreed to sign the
      <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Protocol" title="Boxer Protocol">
       Boxer Protocol
      </a>
      , which stipulated the presence of an international military force in Beijing and the payment of
      <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">
       
      </a>
      67million (almost $333million) in
      <a href="/wiki/War_reparations" title="War reparations">
       war reparations
      </a>
      . The United States used its share of the war indemnity to fund the creation of China's prestigious
      <a href="/wiki/Tsinghua_University" title="Tsinghua University">
       Tsinghua University
      </a>
      . The Emperor and the Empress Dowager did not return to the capital from Xi'an until roughly eighteen months after their flight.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_Beijing_and_Reforms">
       Return to Beijing and Reforms
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Return to Beijing and Reforms">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In January 1902, the Empress Dowager, Emperor, Empress and the rest of the court made a ceremonious return to Beijing. At the railhead at Chengtingfu, Cixi and the court boarded a twenty-one car train to convey them the rest of the way to the capital.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In Beijing, many of the legation women turned out to watch the procession from Beijing's railroad station to the Forbidden City, and for the first time, ordinary Chinese were permitted to watch, as well.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Once back in the palace, Cixi implemented sweeping political reforms. High officials were dispatched to Japan and Europe to gather facts and draw up plans for sweeping administrative reforms in law, education, government structure, and social policy, many of which were modeled on the reforms of the
      <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">
       Meiji Restoration
      </a>
      . The abolition of the examination system in 1905 was only the most visible of these sweeping reforms. Ironically, Cixi sponsored the implementation of the
      <a href="/wiki/New_Policies" title="New Policies">
       New Policies
      </a>
      reform program more radical than the one proposed by the reformers she had beheaded in 1898.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      She also, in an attempt to woo the foreigners, invited the wives of the diplomatic corps to a tea in the Forbidden City soon after her return, and in time, would hold summer garden parties for the foreign community at the Summer Palace. In 1903, she acquiesced to the request of Sarah Conger, wife of
      <a href="/wiki/Edwin_H._Conger" title="Edwin H. Conger">
       Edwin Conger
      </a>
      , the American minister to China, to have her portrait painted by American artist
      <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Carl" title="Katharine Carl">
       Katharine Carl
      </a>
      for the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis_World%27s_Fair" title="St. Louis World's Fair">
       St. Louis World's Fair
      </a>
      . Between 1903 and 1905 Cixi had a western educated lady-in-waiting by the name of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Der_Ling" title="Der Ling">
       Der Ling
      </a>
      , along with her sister and mother, serve at her court. Der Ling, fluent in English and French, as well as Chinese, often served as translator at meetings with the wives of the diplomatic corps.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_by_an_Imperial_Painter_4.PNG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="429" data-file-width="263" height="359" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_by_an_Imperial_Painter_4.PNG/220px-The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_by_an_Imperial_Painter_4.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/ff/The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_by_an_Imperial_Painter_4.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/ff/The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_by_an_Imperial_Painter_4.PNG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_by_an_Imperial_Painter_4.PNG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         Empress Dowager Cixi,
        </i>
        1904, was given to President
        <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">
         Theodore Roosevelt
        </a>
        , who had it added to the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Museum_of_American_Art" title="Smithsonian Museum of American Art">
         Smithsonian Museum of American Art
        </a>
        collections
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
         <a href="#cite_note-46">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          46
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1903, Cixi allowed a young aristocratic photographer named Xunling, a brother of Der Ling, to take elaborately staged shots of her and her court, designed to convey imperial authority, aesthetic refinement, and religious piety. As the only photographic series taken of Cixithe supreme leader of China for more than forty-five yearsit represents a unique convergence of Qing court pictorial traditions, modern photographic techniques, and Western standards of artistic portraiture. The rare glass plates have been blown up into full-size images, included in the exhibition "The Empress Dowager" at the
      <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Sackler_Gallery" title="Arthur M. Sackler Gallery">
       Arthur M. Sackler Gallery
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution" title="Smithsonian Institution">
       Smithsonian Institution
      </a>
      , Washington, D.C.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_final_resting_place">
       Death and final resting place
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Death and final resting place">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cixi_tomb_underground_2011_11.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="2304" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Cixi_tomb_underground_2011_11.jpg/170px-Cixi_tomb_underground_2011_11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Cixi_tomb_underground_2011_11.jpg/255px-Cixi_tomb_underground_2011_11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Cixi_tomb_underground_2011_11.jpg/340px-Cixi_tomb_underground_2011_11.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cixi_tomb_underground_2011_11.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Entrance to the burial chamber in Cixi's tomb
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cixi_tomb_memorial_tower_2011_11.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Cixi_tomb_memorial_tower_2011_11.jpg/220px-Cixi_tomb_memorial_tower_2011_11.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Cixi_tomb_memorial_tower_2011_11.jpg/330px-Cixi_tomb_memorial_tower_2011_11.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Cixi_tomb_memorial_tower_2011_11.jpg/440px-Cixi_tomb_memorial_tower_2011_11.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cixi_tomb_memorial_tower_2011_11.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Memorial tower of the tomb of Empress Dowager Cixi
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Empress Dowager Cixi died in the Hall of Graceful Bird at the Middle Sea (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ) of
      <a href="/wiki/Zhongnanhai" title="Zhongnanhai">
       Zhongnanhai
      </a>
      on 15 November 1908, after having installed
      <a href="/wiki/Puyi" title="Puyi">
       Puyi
      </a>
      as the new Emperor of the
      <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">
       Qing dynasty
      </a>
      on 14
      <span style="white-space:nowrap;">
      </span>
      November. Her death came only a day after the death of the
      <a href="/wiki/Guangxu_Emperor" title="Guangxu Emperor">
       Guangxu Emperor
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      On 4 November 2008, forensic tests concluded that the death of the Emperor was caused by acute arsenic poisoning.
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/China_Daily" title="China Daily">
        China Daily
       </a>
      </i>
      quoted a historian,
      <a href="/wiki/Dai_Yi" title="Dai Yi">
       Dai Yi
      </a>
      , who speculated that Cixi may have known of her imminent death and may have worried that Guangxu would continue his reforms after her death.
      <a href="/wiki/CNN" title="CNN">
       CNN
      </a>
      reported in November 2008 that the level of arsenic in his remains was 2,000 times higher than that of ordinary people.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Empress Dowager Cixi was interred amidst the
      <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Qing_tombs" title="Eastern Qing tombs">
       Eastern Qing tombs
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ), 125km (78mi) east of Beijing, in the Dong Dingling (
      <span lang="zh-hans" xml:lang="zh-hans">
       
      </span>
      ), along with Empress Dowager Ci'an. More precisely, Empress Dowager Ci'an lies in the Pu Xiang Yu Ding Dong Ling (
      <span lang="zh-hans" xml:lang="zh-hans">
       
      </span>
      ) (literally: the "Tomb East of the Ding Ling Tomb in the Broad Valley of Good Omen"), while Empress Dowager Cixi built herself the much larger Pu Tuo Yu Ding Dong Ling (
      <span lang="zh-hans" xml:lang="zh-hans">
       
      </span>
      ) (literally: the "Tomb East of the Ding Ling Tomb in the Potala Valley"). The Dingling tomb (literally: the "Tomb of quietude") is the tomb of the Xianfeng Emperor, the spouse of
      <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Ci%27an" title="Empress Dowager Ci'an">
       Empress Dowager Ci'an
      </a>
      and Empress Dowager Cixi, which is located indeed west of the Ding Dong Ling. The Putuo Valley owes its name to
      <a href="/wiki/Mount_Putuo" title="Mount Putuo">
       Mount Putuo
      </a>
      , one of the
      <a href="/wiki/Sacred_Mountains_of_China" title="Sacred Mountains of China">
       Four Sacred Buddhist Mountains of China
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Empress Dowager Cixi, unsatisfied with her tomb, ordered its destruction and reconstruction in 1895. The new tomb was a lavish grandiose complex of temples, gates, and pavilions, covered with gold leaf, and with gold and gilded-bronze ornaments hanging from the beams and the eaves. In July 1928, Empress Dowager Cixi's tomb
      <a href="/wiki/Looting_of_the_Eastern_Mausoleum" title="Looting of the Eastern Mausoleum">
       was occupied
      </a>
      by warlord and
      <a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">
       Kuomintang
      </a>
      general
      <a href="/wiki/Sun_Dianying" title="Sun Dianying">
       Sun Dianying
      </a>
      and his army who methodically stripped the complex of its precious ornaments, then dynamited the entrance to the burial chamber, opened Empress Dowager Cixi's coffin, threw her corpse (said to have been found intact) on the floor, and stole all the jewels contained in the coffin, as well as the massive pearl that had been placed in Empress Dowager Cixi's mouth to protect her corpse from decomposing (in accordance with Chinese tradition). Urban legend states that the large pearl on Empress Dowager Cixi's crown was offered by Sun Dianying to Kuomintang leader
      <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Kai-shek" title="Chiang Kai-shek">
       Chiang Kai-shek
      </a>
      and ended up as an ornament on the gala shoes of Chiang's wife,
      <a href="/wiki/Soong_May-ling" title="Soong May-ling">
       Soong May-ling
      </a>
      , but this is unconfirmed.
     </p>
     <p>
      After 1949, the complex of Empress Dowager Cixi's tomb was restored by the People's Republic of China, and it is still today one of the most impressive imperial tombs of China.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Family">
       Family
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Family">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Paternal great-grandfather
       <ul>
        <li>
         Yehenara Jilang A ()
        </li>
       </ul>
      </li>
      <li>
       Paternal grandfather
       <ul>
        <li>
         Yehenara Jingrui ()
        </li>
       </ul>
      </li>
      <li>
       Father
       <ul>
        <li>
         Yehenara Huizheng () (29 September 1805 ?), Manchu official of the Blue Bordered Banner, served in
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shanxi_Province" title="Shanxi Province">
          Shanxi Province
         </a>
         before becoming Commissioner of
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anhui_Province" title="Anhui Province">
          Anhui
         </a>
        </li>
       </ul>
      </li>
      <li>
       Mother
       <ul>
        <li>
         Lady Fuca (), Huizheng's primary wife, daughter of Fuca Huixian ()
        </li>
       </ul>
      </li>
      <li>
       Mate
       <ul>
        <li>
         <a href="/wiki/Xianfeng_Emperor" title="Xianfeng Emperor">
          Xianfeng Emperor
         </a>
         , father of the
         <a href="/wiki/Tongzhi_Emperor" title="Tongzhi Emperor">
          Tongzhi Emperor
         </a>
        </li>
       </ul>
      </li>
      <li>
       Son
       <ul>
        <li>
         <a href="/wiki/Tongzhi_Emperor" title="Tongzhi Emperor">
          Tongzhi Emperor
         </a>
        </li>
       </ul>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Daughter (by adoption)
       <ul>
        <li>
         Princess Rongshou
        </li>
       </ul>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>

       Siblings and their descendants
      </span>

        [
       </span>

        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E9%86%87%E4%BA%B2%E7%8E%8B%E7%A6%8F%E6%99%8B%E5%90%88%E5%BD%B1.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="904" data-file-width="1108" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/%E9%86%87%E4%BA%B2%E7%8E%8B%E7%A6%8F%E6%99%8B%E5%90%88%E5%BD%B1.jpg/220px-%E9%86%87%E4%BA%B2%E7%8E%8B%E7%A6%8F%E6%99%8B%E5%90%88%E5%BD%B1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/%E9%86%87%E4%BA%B2%E7%8E%8B%E7%A6%8F%E6%99%8B%E5%90%88%E5%BD%B1.jpg/330px-%E9%86%87%E4%BA%B2%E7%8E%8B%E7%A6%8F%E6%99%8B%E5%90%88%E5%BD%B1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/%E9%86%87%E4%BA%B2%E7%8E%8B%E7%A6%8F%E6%99%8B%E5%90%88%E5%BD%B1.jpg/440px-%E9%86%87%E4%BA%B2%E7%8E%8B%E7%A6%8F%E6%99%8B%E5%90%88%E5%BD%B1.jpg 2x" width="220"/>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Names_of_Empress_Dowager_Cixi">
       Names of Empress Dowager Cixi
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Names of Empress Dowager Cixi">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SC-GR-257.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="596" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/SC-GR-257.jpg/170px-SC-GR-257.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/SC-GR-257.jpg/255px-SC-GR-257.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/SC-GR-257.jpg/340px-SC-GR-257.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SC-GR-257.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The plaque hanging above Cixi is inscribed with her title in full
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empress_Dowager_Cixi_on_the_imperial_barge_on_Zhonghai.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1491" data-file-width="2000" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Empress_Dowager_Cixi_on_the_imperial_barge_on_Zhonghai.jpg/220px-Empress_Dowager_Cixi_on_the_imperial_barge_on_Zhonghai.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Empress_Dowager_Cixi_on_the_imperial_barge_on_Zhonghai.jpg/330px-Empress_Dowager_Cixi_on_the_imperial_barge_on_Zhonghai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Empress_Dowager_Cixi_on_the_imperial_barge_on_Zhonghai.jpg/440px-Empress_Dowager_Cixi_on_the_imperial_barge_on_Zhonghai.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Empress_Dowager_Cixi_on_the_imperial_barge_on_Zhonghai.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The Empress Dowager was a devoted Buddhist and seized every opportunity to dress up as Avalokitevara, the goddess of mercy. This photograph shows her sitting on a barge on Zhonghai. The white smoke forms the character for longevity, and on top of the smoke was her Buddhist name "Guangrenzi" (literally
        <i>
         Universal Benevolence
        </i>
        ).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The name by which she is most frequently known and the name used in most modern texts is simply "Cixi", which is neither her birth name nor family name. It is an "honorific name" given to her in 1861 after her son ascended the throne. Empress Dowager Cixi's name at birth is not known, although a recent book published by one of Cixi's brother's descendants seems to suggest that it was
      <i>
       Xingzhen
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
       pinyin
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
        Xngzhn
       </span>
      </i>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="WadeGiles">
       WadeGiles
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile" xml:lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">
        Hsingchen
       </span>
      </i>
      ). The first occurrence of her name is at the time she entered the
      <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">
       Forbidden City
      </a>
      in September 1851, where she was recorded as
      <i>
       "the Lady Yehenara, daughter of Huizheng"
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
       pinyin
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
        Hu Zhng
       </span>
      </i>
      ). Thus, she was called by her clan's name, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nara_(clan)" title="Nara (clan)">
       Yehe-Nara
      </a>
      clan, as was customary for Manchu girls. On entering the
      <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">
       Forbidden City
      </a>
      , she was a preparative concubine (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
       pinyin
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
        xi n
       </span>
      </i>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      After her sexual union with the
      <a href="/wiki/Xianfeng_Emperor" title="Xianfeng Emperor">
       Xianfeng Emperor
      </a>
      , she was made a concubine of the fifth rank
      <i>
       Noble Person
      </i>
      , a.k.a. "Worthy Lady" (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
       pinyin
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
        Gurn
       </span>
      </i>
      ), and was given the name Yi (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
       pinyin
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
        Y
       </span>
      </i>
      , "good, exemplary or virtuous"). Her name was thus "Noble Person of Yi", or
      <i>
       Worthy Lady Yi
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
       pinyin
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
        Y Gurn
       </span>
      </i>
      ). At the end of December 1854 or the beginning of January 1855, she was promoted to concubine of the fourth rank,
      <i>
       Imperial Concubine
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
       pinyin
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
        Pn
       </span>
      </i>
      ), so that her new name was
      <i>
       Imperial Concubine Yi
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      On 27 April 1856, Yehenara gave birth to a son, the only son of
      <a href="/wiki/Xianfeng_Emperor" title="Xianfeng Emperor">
       Xianfeng
      </a>
      , and was immediately made
      <i>
       Noble Consort Yi"
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
       pinyin
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
        Y Fi
       </span>
      </i>
      ). Finally, in February 1857 she was again elevated and made "Noble Imperial Consort Yi" (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
       pinyin
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
        Y Gufi
       </span>
      </i>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      In the end of August 1861, following the death of the Xianfeng Emperor, her five-year-old son became the new Emperor, known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Tongzhi_Emperor" title="Tongzhi Emperor">
       Tongzhi Emperor
      </a>
      . Empress Dowager Cixi, as biological mother of the new emperor, was officially made
      <i>
       Divine Mother Empress Dowager
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
       pinyin
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
        Shngm Hung Tihu
       </span>
      </i>
      ). She was also given the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honorific_name" title="Honorific name">
       honorific name
      </a>
      Cixi (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
       pinyin
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
        Cx
       </span>
      </i>
      ), meaning "Motherly and Auspicious". As for the Empress Consort, she was made
      <i>
       "Mother Empress Dowager"
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
       pinyin
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
        Mhu Hung Tihu
       </span>
      </i>
      ), a title giving her precedence over Empress Dowager Cixi, and she was given the honorific name
      <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Ci%27an" title="Empress Dowager Ci'an">
       Empress Dowager Ci'an
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
       pinyin
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
        C'n
       </span>
      </i>
      ) meaning "Motherly and Calm".
     </p>
     <p>
      On 7 occasions after 1861, Empress Dowager Cixi was given additional honorific names (two Chinese characters at a time), as was customary for emperors and empresses, until by the end of her reign her name was a long string of 16 characters starting with Cixi (as empress dowager she had the right to nine additions, giving a total of 20 characters, had she lived long enough for it). At the end of her life, her official name was:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
        Chinese
       </a>
       :
       <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
        
       </span>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
        pinyin
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
         D qng gu dngjn cx dun yu kngyzho y zhung chngshu gng qn xin chng x shngm hung tihu
        </span>
       </i>
       ) which reads:
       <i>
        "The Current Divine Mother Empress Dowager Ci-Xi Duan-You Kang-Yi Zhao-Yu Zhuang-Cheng Shou-Gong Qin-Xian Chong-Xi of the Great Qing Empire"
       </i>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       The short form was
       <i>
        The Current Divine Mother Empress Dowager of the Great Qing Empire
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
        Chinese
       </a>
       :
       <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
        
       </span>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
        pinyin
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
         D qng gu dngjn shngm hung tihu
        </span>
       </i>
       )
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      At the time, Empress Dowager Cixi was addressed as
      <i>
       "Venerable Buddha"
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
       pinyin
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
        Lo Fy
       </span>
      </i>
      )literally "Master Old Buddha", a term used for all the Emperors of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" title="Qing Dynasty">
       Qing Dynasty
      </a>
      . At official and ceremonial occasions, the phrase
      <i>
       Long Live the Empress Dowager for ten thousand years
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
       Chinese
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
       pinyin
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
        D qng gu dngjn shngm hung tihu wn su wn su wn wn su
       </span>
      </i>
      ), which is by convention, only used by emperors. The convention for empress dowagers of imperial China was usually
      <i>
       Long live for one thousand years
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      At her death in 1908, Empress Dowager Cixi was given a
      <a href="/wiki/Posthumous_name" title="Posthumous name">
       posthumous name
      </a>
      which combines the honorific names that she gained during her lifetime with new names added just after her death. This is the name that is usually used on official documents to refer to an empress. This long form of the posthumous name is:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
        Chinese
       </a>
       :
       <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
        
       </span>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
        pinyin
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
         Xio qn cx dun yu kngyzho y zhung chngshu gng qn xin chng x pi tin xng shng xin hung tihu
        </span>
       </i>
       ), which reads:
       <i>
        Empress Xiao-Qin Ci-Xi Duan-You Kang-Yi Zhao-Yu Zhuang-Cheng Shou-Gong Qin-Xian Chong-Xi Pei-Tian Xing-Sheng Xian
       </i>
       . This long name is still the one that can be seen on Cixi's tomb today. The short form of her posthumous name is:
       <i>
        Empress Xiao Qin Xian
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
        Chinese
       </a>
       :
       <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
        
       </span>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
        pinyin
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
         Xio Qn Xin Hunghu
        </span>
       </i>
       ).
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_opinions">
       Historical opinions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Historical opinions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_in_the_1900s.PNG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="567" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_in_the_1900s.PNG/170px-The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_in_the_1900s.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_in_the_1900s.PNG/255px-The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_in_the_1900s.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_in_the_1900s.PNG/340px-The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_in_the_1900s.PNG 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Portrait_of_the_Qing_Dynasty_Cixi_Imperial_Dowager_Empress_of_China_in_the_1900s.PNG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        One of the historical oil paintings by Western artists depicting Empress Dowager Cixi
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The dominant historical view of the Empress Dowager Cixi was that of a devious
      <a href="/wiki/Despotism" title="Despotism">
       despot
      </a>
      who contributed in no small part to China's slide into corruption, anarchy, and revolution. During Cixi's time, she used her power to accumulate vast quantities of money, bullion, antiques and jewelry, using the revenues of the state as her own. However, the most problematic points in Chinese history happened when she was in "retirement" such as when her nephew focused on studying Confucian texts instead of building up the army at the same time that Japan was amassing its navy. When the
      <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">
       First Sino-Japanese War
      </a>
      became untenable, Cixi came out of retirement in order to clean up her nephew's shoddy administration. The lavish palaces, gardens and lakes built by Cixi were hugely extravagant at a time when China was verging on bankruptcy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Katharine_Carl">
       Katharine Carl
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Katharine Carl">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empress_Dowager_portrait.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="769" data-file-width="466" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Empress_Dowager_portrait.jpg/170px-Empress_Dowager_portrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Empress_Dowager_portrait.jpg/255px-Empress_Dowager_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Empress_Dowager_portrait.jpg/340px-Empress_Dowager_portrait.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Empress_Dowager_portrait.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Katharine Carl's oil painting of Cixi
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Carl" title="Katharine Carl">
       Katharine Carl
      </a>
      spent some ten months with the Empress Dowager Cixi in 1903 to paint her portrait for the
      <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase_Exposition" title="Louisiana Purchase Exposition">
       St. Louis Exposition
      </a>
      . Two years later she published a book about her experience, titled
      <i>
       With the Empress Dowager
      </i>
      . In the book's introduction, Carl says she wrote the book because "After I returned to America, I was constantly seeing in the newspapers (and hearing of) statements ascribed to me which I never made."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In her book, Katharine Carl describes the Empress Dowager Cixi as a kind and considerate woman for her station. Empress Dowager Cixi, though shrewd, had great presence, charm, and graceful movements resulting in "an unusually attractive personality". Carl wrote of the Dowager's love of dogs and of flowers, as well as boating,
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_opera" title="Chinese opera">
       Chinese opera
      </a>
      and her Chinese water pipes and European cigarettes. Carl also made note of Empress Dowager Cixi's loyalty, describing the case of "a Chinese woman who nursed Her Majesty through a long illness, about twenty-five years since, and saved her life by giving her mother's milk to drink. Her Majesty, who never forgets a favor, has always kept this woman in the Palace. Being a Chinese, she had
      <a href="/wiki/Foot_binding" title="Foot binding">
       bound feet
      </a>
      . Her Majesty, who cannot bear to see them even, had her feet unbound and carefully treated, until now she can walk comfortably. Her Majesty has educated the son, who was an infant at the time of her illness, and whose natural nourishment she partook of. This young man is already a Secretary in a good
      <a href="/wiki/Yamen" title="Yamen">
       yamen
      </a>
      (government office)."
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Luke_Kwong">
       Luke Kwong
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Luke Kwong">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Luke Kwong, in his analysis of the Hundred Days of Reform, has argued that many of the allegations of being power-hungry and immoral cannot be verified.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kwong.2C_pg._29_23-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kwong.2C_pg._29-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He also portrays her as a relatively insecure woman, concerned about her legitimacy and haunted by her relatively humble origins in the palace.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Yet, despite her concerns about her legitimacy, she was not necessarily power-hungry tyrant who manipulated the court, but was rather content to remain as merely part of a coalition government between the herself and the Grand Council, as well as a dynastic figurehead, so long as her sense of legitimacy was respected and unchallenged.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He also argues that her return to power in 1898 was driven less by Cixi's desire to gain power and opposition to reform, as she had been privy to dispatches from the Grand Council and consulted by the emperor, but that her detachment from court politics
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kwong.2C_pg._203_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kwong.2C_pg._203-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and tendency to rely on second-hand accounts had made her subject to manipulation by her informants
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      to the point that she felt it urgent
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kwong.2C_pg._203_53-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kwong.2C_pg._203-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      to resume her "tutelage" of the emperor.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sterling_Seagrave">
       Sterling Seagrave
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Sterling Seagrave">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Seagrave, argues
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      that most of the more sensational stories of Empress Dowager Cixi's life can be traced to the boasting, self-important "Wild Fox"
      <a href="/wiki/Kang_Youwei" title="Kang Youwei">
       Kang Youwei
      </a>
      and his cronies who, never having met the Empress Dowager, concocted stories of plots and poisonings and passed them on to the Western press. Many other "details" of her life are based on accounts by J. O. P. Bland and known forger
      <a href="/wiki/Sir_Edmund_Backhouse,_2nd_Baronet" title="Sir Edmund Backhouse, 2nd Baronet">
       Edmund Backhouse
      </a>
      . As life in the Forbidden City remained a mystery for most Westerners, these stories created by Kang and Backhouse (some up to 30 years after the supposed events) were used by many 20th-century historians to paint a misleading picture of the Empress Dowager.
     </p>
     <p>
      In contrast, Seagrave portrays Empress Dowager Cixi as a woman stuck between the xenophobic Ironhats faction, made up of Manchu nobility wanting to maintain Manchu dominance and remove Western influences from China at all cost, and more moderate influences trying to cope with China's problems on a more realistic footing, such as Prince Gong in Cixi's earlier days. The Empress Dowager, Seagrave argues, did not crave power but simply acted to balance these influences and protect the Dynasty as best she could.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Lei_Chia-sheng">
       Lei Chia-sheng
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Lei Chia-sheng">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      According to research by Professor Lei Chia-sheng (),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      during the
      <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days%27_Reform" title="Hundred Days' Reform">
       Hundred Days' Reform
      </a>
      (), former prime minister of Japan
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ito_Hirobumi" title="Ito Hirobumi">
       It Hirobumi
      </a>
      () arrived in China on 11 September 1898. Almost at the same time, British missionary Timothy Richard was invited to Beijing by Kang Youwei. Richard suggested that China should hand over some political power to It in order to help push the reforms further.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On 18 September, Richard convinced Kang Youwei to adopt a plan by which China would join a federation composed of China, Japan, the United States, and England. This suggestion did not reflect the policies of the countries concerned. It was Timothy Richards (and perhaps It Hirobumi's) trick to convince China to hand over national rights. Kang Youwei nonetheless asked fellow reformers Yang Shenxiu () and Song Bolu () to report this plan to the Guangxu Emperor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On 20 September, Yang sent a memorial to this effect to the Emperor.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In another memorial written the next day, Song Bolu also advocated the formation of a federation and the sharing of the diplomatic, fiscal, and military powers of the four countries under a hundred-man committee.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Still according to Lei Chia-sheng's findings, on 13 October, British ambassador Sir C. MacDonald reported to his government about the Chinese situation, saying that Chinese reforms had been damaged by Kang Youwei and his friends actions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      British diplomat Baurne claimed in his own report that Kang was a dreamer who had been seduced by Timothy Richards sweet words. Baurne thought Richard was a plotter.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The British and American governments were unaware of the "federation" plot, which seems to have been Timothy Richards personal idea. Because Richard's partner It Hirobumi had been Prime Minister of Japan, the Japanese government might have known about Richard's plan, but there is no evidence to this effect.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Princess_Der_Ling">
       Princess Der Ling
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Princess Der Ling">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Der_Ling" title="Der Ling">
       Der Ling
      </a>
      , whose Christian name was Elisabeth Antoinette, was born in Beijing in June 1885 and died in Berkeley, California in November 1944. She was the eldest daughter of Yu Keng, an official of the Chinese-Martial (Han Jun) Plain White Banner, and his wife, Louisa Pierson, daughter of an American merchant in Shanghai and his Chinese consort.
     </p>
     <p>
      When Der Ling's father was recalled from Paris, where he had been a Chinese minister, in 1903, Der Ling, her sister Rong Ling (later the wife of General Dan Paochao) and their mother were summoned by Cixi to become court ladies  something between ladies-in-waiting and translators/hostesses for when the Empress Dowager had foreign female guests from Beijing's Legation Quarter.
     </p>
     <p>
      Der Ling served at court from March 1903 till October 1905, and married an American, Thaddeus Cohu White, in 1907.
     </p>
     <p>
      After Cixi's death in 1908, Der Ling professed to be so angered by what she saw as false portraits of Cixi appearing in books and periodicals that she wrote her own account of serving "Old Buddha", which she called "Two Years in the Forbidden City". This book appeared in 1911, just before the fall of the Qing Dynasty, and was a popular success.
     </p>
     <p>
      In this book, Cixi is not the monster of depravity depicted in the popular press and in the second and third hand accounts left by foreigners who had lived in Beijing, but an aging woman who loved beautiful things, had many regrets about the past and the way she had dealt with the many crises of her long reign, and apparently trusted Der Ling enough to share many memories and opinions with her.
     </p>
     <p>
      It was clearly Cixi's favouritism toward Der Ling, including permitting her to wear a "princess button" on her hat, that prompted Der Ling in later years, when seeking an English equivalent to her office at court, to add "Princess" to her name, a move that undermined her credibility in China even as it drove up her stock when she went before the American public in the 1920s to give lectures about life at court with the semi-legendary Cixi. Der Ling ultimately wrote a full-length biography of Cixi titled 'Old Buddha.'
     </p>
     <p>
      Der Ling would go on to write seven more books about this relatively brief period in her youth when she had been close to the centre of failing imperial Chinese power, and sharing this personal history and her habit of promoting herself and her writings caused most of her family to turn against her. All of this has made it difficult to assess Der Ling's contribution to late Qing historiography. But the fact remains that she was the first woman of Cixi's own ethnic background to live with and observe her and then write about what it was like; if many of Der Ling's recollections smack of the every day minutiae of a court that thrived on details and form, her writings are no less valuable for focusing on them, particularly as life within the Forbidden City and the Summer Palace was a closed book for most people in China, let alone in the rest of the world. It was misunderstanding of much of what emanated from the throne that created so many of the problems Cixi has been wholly blamed for.
     </p>
     <p>
      Starting with Sterling Seagrave's biography of Cixi, 'Dragon Lady: The Life and Legend of the Last Empress of China', Der Ling and her reminiscence of the imperial court have been rehabilitated in recent years, in tandem with reassessments of the Empress Dowager herself. In January 2008, Hong Kong University Press published the first biography of Der Ling, 'Imperial Masquerade: The Legend of Princess Der Ling'.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Jung_Chang">
       Jung Chang
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Jung Chang">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 2013, Jung Chang's biography of the empress dowager,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Cixi:_The_Concubine_Who_Launched_Modern_China" title="Empress Dowager Cixi: The Concubine Who Launched Modern China">
        Empress Dowager Cixi: The Concubine Who Launched Modern China
       </a>
      </i>
      , set out to correct the negative history. Chang portrays Cixi as the most capable ruler and administrator that China could have had at the time. Katie Baker wrote in
      <a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Beast" title="The Daily Beast">
       The Daily Beast
      </a>
      "There have indeed been many judgments passed on Cixi's legacy, from the rumors (possibly spread by Wild Kang) that she was a sexually voracious despot to Changs final pronouncement that "the past hundred years have been most unfair to Cixi" and that "the political forces that have dominated China since soon after her death have also deliberately reviled her or blacked out her accomplishments[but] in terms of groundbreaking achievements, political sincerity and personal courage, Empress Dowager Cixi set a standard that has barely been matched."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thedailybeast.com_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-thedailybeast.com-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Historian
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pamela_Crossley" title="Pamela Crossley">
       Pamela Crossley
      </a>
      , however, cautions that Chang Jung "does not merely repeat what are now truisms in the representation of Cixi  that she has been obscured by misogyny and orientalist stereotyping, as well as the anti-Manchu sentiment running through Chinese nationalist narratives  but also claims to have discovered something new." Crossley says that these claims regarding Cixi's importance "seem to be minted from her own musings, and have little to do with what was actually going on in China." She faults the book for crediting Cixi with powers that were in fact held elsewhere.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_sources">
       Other sources
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Other sources">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Cixi appears frequently in ceremonies described in the diaries of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Ernest_Satow" title="Sir Ernest Satow">
       Sir Ernest Satow
      </a>
      for 190006 when Satow was British envoy in Peking.
     </p>
     <p>
      Another well known but now widely questioned biography is "China Under The Empress Dowager" by J. O. P. Bland and
      <a href="/wiki/Sir_Edmund_Backhouse,_2nd_Baronet" title="Sir Edmund Backhouse, 2nd Baronet">
       Edmund Backhouse
      </a>
      . Backhouse was later found to have forged some of his source materials when he wrote this work. This is a book that gave rise to much of the negative perspective of the dowager empress.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Honours">
       Honours
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Honours">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JPN_Hokan-sho_1Class_BAR.svg">
        <img alt="JPN Hokan-sho 1Class BAR.svg" data-file-height="60" data-file-width="218" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/JPN_Hokan-sho_1Class_BAR.svg/50px-JPN_Hokan-sho_1Class_BAR.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/JPN_Hokan-sho_1Class_BAR.svg/75px-JPN_Hokan-sho_1Class_BAR.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/JPN_Hokan-sho_1Class_BAR.svg/100px-JPN_Hokan-sho_1Class_BAR.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
       </a>
       Dame Grand Cordon of the
       <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Precious_Crown" title="Order of the Precious Crown">
        Order of the Precious Crown
       </a>
       (Empire of Japan).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         67
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">
       In popular culture
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Empress_Dowager_Cixi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: In popular culture">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Der_Ling" title="Der Ling">
        Der Ling
       </a>
       's story
       <i>
        The True Story of the Empress Dowager
       </i>
       (originally published as
       <i>
        Old Buddha
       </i>
       ) reveals the history and true story behind the character of the Empress-Dowager Cixinot the monster of depravity depicted in the popular press. but an aging woman who loved beautiful things and had many regrets about the past. (Soul Care Publishing, 2015)
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Pearl_S._Buck" title="Pearl S. Buck">
        Pearl S. Buck
       </a>
       's novel
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Woman" title="Imperial Woman">
         Imperial Woman
        </a>
       </i>
       chronicles the life of the Empress Dowager from the time of her selection as a concubine until near to her death.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Bette_Bao_Lord" title="Bette Bao Lord">
        Bette Bao Lord
       </a>
       's novel
       <i>
        Spring Moon
       </i>
       starts in the days of Cixi, and includes the involvement of the Imperial Court in the
       <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Rebellion" title="Boxer Rebellion">
        Boxer Rebellion
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       The novels
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Empress_Orchid" title="Empress Orchid">
         Empress Orchid
        </a>
       </i>
       (2004) and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Last_Empress_(novel)" title="The Last Empress (novel)">
         The Last Empress
        </a>
       </i>
       (2007), by
       <a href="/wiki/Anchee_Min" title="Anchee Min">
        Anchee Min
       </a>
       portray the life of Empress Dowager Cixi from a first-person perspective.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Jung_Chang" title="Jung Chang">
        Jung Chang
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Cixi:_The_Concubine_Who_Launched_Modern_China" title="Empress Dowager Cixi: The Concubine Who Launched Modern China">
         Empress Dowager Cixi: The Concubine Who Launched Modern China
        </a>
       </i>
       (New York: Alfred A. Knopf) appeared in 2013.
      </li>
      <li>
       The Noble Concubine Yi is featured in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_McDonald_Fraser" title="George McDonald Fraser">
        George McDonald Fraser
       </a>
       's novel,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Flashman_and_the_Dragon" title="Flashman and the Dragon">
         Flashman and the Dragon
        </a>
       </i>
       (1985).
      </li>
      <li>
       The 1968 novel
       <i>
        Wij Tz'e Hsi Keizerin Van China
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        We, Tz'e Hsi, Empress of China
       </i>
       ) by Dutch author
       <a href="/wiki/Johan_Fabricius" title="Johan Fabricius">
        Johan Fabricius
       </a>
       is a fictional diary of the Empress.
      </li>
      <li>
       Cixi is portrayed by British actress
       <a href="/wiki/Flora_Robson" title="Flora Robson">
        Flora Robson
       </a>
       in the 1963 film
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/55_Days_At_Peking" title="55 Days At Peking">
         55 Days At Peking
        </a>
        .
       </i>
      </li>
      <li>
       In the 1970s, she was portrayed by
       <a href="/wiki/Lisa_Lu" title="Lisa Lu">
        Lisa Lu
       </a>
       in two Hong Kong-made films,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Empress_Dowager" title="The Empress Dowager">
         The Empress Dowager
        </a>
       </i>
       (set during the Sino-Japanese War) and its sequel,
       <i>
        The Last Tempest
       </i>
       (set during the "Hundred Days of Reform").
      </li>
      <li>
       Lu reprised her role as Cixi in the 1987 film
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Last_Emperor" title="The Last Emperor">
         The Last Emperor
        </a>
       </i>
       , depicting the dowager on her deathbed.
      </li>
      <li>
       In the 1980s, she was portrayed by
       <a href="/wiki/Liu_Xiaoqing" title="Liu Xiaoqing">
        Liu Xiaoqing
       </a>
       , in
       <i>
        Burning of Imperial Palace
       </i>
       (depicting her rise to power in the 1850's, and the burning of the Old Summer Palace by French and British troops in 1860), in
       <i>
        Reign Behind a Curtain
       </i>
       (depicting the Xinyou Coup of 1861), in
       <i>
        The Empress Dowager
       </i>
       (set during the latter part of the reign of Tongzhi), and in
       <i>
        Li Lianying, the Imperial Eunuch.
       </i>
      </li>
      <li>
       In the
       <i>
        Lover of the Last Empress
       </i>
       , she was portrayed by
       <a href="/wiki/Chingmy_Yau" title="Chingmy Yau">
        Chingmy Yau
       </a>
       .
      </li>
      <li>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/China_Central_Television" title="China Central Television">
        China Central Television
       </a>
       production
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Towards_the_Republic" title="Towards the Republic">
         Towards the Republic
        </a>
       </i>
       portrayed Empress Dowager Cixi as a capable ruler, the first time that Mainland Chinese television had shown her in this light; the portrayal was not entirely positive, as it also clearly demonstrated her political views to be very conservative.
      </li>
     </ul>

         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AEmpress+Dowager+Cixi&amp;rft.au=Chester+C.+Tan&amp;rft.aulast=Chester+C.+Tan&amp;rft.btitle=The+Boxer+catastrophe&amp;rft.date=1967&amp;rft.edition=reprint&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fei%3DoGsLT5rpEqHu0gGY29nuBQ%26id%3D_gcOAQAAMAAJ%26dq%3Dhave%2Bstarted%2Bthe%2Baggression%252C%2Band%2Bthe%2Bextinction%2Bof%2Bour%2Bnation%2Bis%2Bimminent%2B%2Bno%2Bface%2Bancestors%2Bdeath%26q%3Dextinction%2Bnation&amp;rft.isbn=0-374-97752-6&amp;rft.issue=Issue+583+of+Columbia+studies+in+the+social+sciences&amp;rft.pages=73&amp;rft.pub=Octagon+Books&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">

       <li id="cite_note-38">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-38">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation book">
          Marilyn Blatt Young (1969).
          <a class="external text" href="http://books.google.com/books?ei=oGsLT5rpEqHu0gGY29nuBQ&amp;id=tUlCAAAAIAAJ&amp;dq=have+started+the+aggression%2C+and+the+extinction+of+our+nation+is+imminent++no+face+ancestors+death&amp;q=extinction+imminent" rel="nofollow">
           <i>
            The rhetoric of empire: American China policy, 18951901
           </i>
          </a>
          . Harvard University Press. p.147.
          <q>
           a surrender of sovereignty: (1) a special place to be assigned to the emperor for residence; (2) all revenues to be collected by the foreign ministers; (3) all military affairs to be committed to their hands. . .After reading them out the empress dowager declare, "Now they [the powers] have started the aggression, and the extinction of our nation is imminent. If we just fold our arms and yield to them, I would have no face to see our ancestors after death. If we must perish, why not fight to the death?
          </q>
         </span>
